<div id="review_1567301730" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1567301730" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1567301730?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to El by: </span>
      The Roundtable
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-late">20th-centurylit-late</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=library-borrow">library-borrow</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1567301730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer183622463331801044">First off, this is an incredibly hard book for me to rate and review. It started out so strong, I really loved everything about it and couldn't wait to get to know each of the characters in more detail. And there are quite a few characters. The Pickle family and the Lamb family. They come together in an unexpected way when the Pickles move into a large house called Cloudstreet thanks to an inheritance, and because they are poor, they take on the Lambs as tenants. The two families are rather diss</span>
  <span id="freeText183622463331801044" style="display:none">First off, this is an incredibly hard book for me to rate and review. It started out so strong, I really loved everything about it and couldn't wait to get to know each of the characters in more detail. And there are quite a few characters. The Pickle family and the Lamb family. They come together in an unexpected way when the Pickles move into a large house called Cloudstreet thanks to an inheritance, and because they are poor, they take on the Lambs as tenants. The two families are rather dissimilar, except they both are experiencing hard times and, of course, have familial issues that threaten their existences in ways that many families experience in their lives.<br /><br />It's billed as a family saga, and I agree with that. It certainly is. It spans about twenty years of the lives of the Pickles and the Lambs, and we get to know each of the family members fairly well, though I think some characters stand out more strongly than others. I felt close to Rose, for example, throughout the book, whereas others, such as Quick, didn't really feel like a whole person until later on in the book.<br /><br />As I mentioned, the beginning started out strong for me, but by the middle of the book I found myself frustrated and not caring so much as to what was going on. Originally I liked how the story was told in bite-sized pieces which made it read quickly because you could pause easily wherever you wanted and pick it up and delve into another part of the story without really losing speed. By the middle of the book, however, I found it irritating and I <i>wanted</i> to spend more time with the characters, I wasn't making any connection to them anymore.<br /><br />And then by the end of the novel as characters started coming back together in more ways than one, I found myself engrossed again and not wanting to put it down. It was a strange switch, and I couldn't tell you where in the story it started and stopped. But it was there, giving me trouble connecting with the story in an overall sense, even though I so much had wanted to when I started. It's that middle of the book - I'm side-eyeing it now. I can't really trust the middle of this book.<br /><br />But this is still a story that will stick with me because it is powerfully written. Winton is a powerful writer if this is any true indication, and I will be reading more of his work to find out for sure how I feel about him. It's an Australian novel and it is Australian in prose (I swear this is a thing, I felt it with Patrick White as well), dialect, and slang. I loved that because it felt more authentic, even if I found myself chuckling at some of the words. Silly Aussies!<br /><br />I love the little secrets of this novel, the way it gives voice to the disabled and the working class of mid-century Australia. I want to hug many of these characters, and throw things at some of the others. No one is all good and no one is all bad, which is really what life is about anyway. We have this idea of people, often the people in our own families, and we're not always right, our memories aren't always right, and our perceptions aren't always right. Winton really hit the nail on that particular head with this book.<br /><br />Anyway, I liked the book but can't say I loved it. Like I said, it's hard to rate and review. I'm glad to have read it, but I'm not sure who to recommend it to because it's definitely not for everyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="183622463331801044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_590991312" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/590991312" />
    <a title="Christopher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4678382-christopher"><img alt="Christopher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442965554p2/4678382.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/590991312?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher" href="/user/show/4678382-christopher">Christopher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4678382-christopher?shelf=lit-fiction">lit-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer590991312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7805511654183261505">I really had to waffle around in considering how to rate this. There's really some of the best gritty, realistic and poetic writing I've ever read interspersed with some moments of ham-fisted "wise-dickery" (to use Winton's own word). I had to put the book aside three times when I first started it but once I really got into it, I couldn't put it down. Soon though, as it progressed, I gradually lost interest and found myself crawling to the finish line. I never developed anything more than a curs</span>
  <span id="freeText7805511654183261505" style="display:none">I really had to waffle around in considering how to rate this. There's really some of the best gritty, realistic and poetic writing I've ever read interspersed with some moments of ham-fisted "wise-dickery" (to use Winton's own word). I had to put the book aside three times when I first started it but once I really got into it, I couldn't put it down. Soon though, as it progressed, I gradually lost interest and found myself crawling to the finish line. I never developed anything more than a cursory care for what should be very interesting characters. And this is the reason I'm giving it 3 stars. It's the "anti-Willa Cather" novel in the sense that Cather was able to tell an epic and dramatic story of a people that took place over decades and generations (like in <i>O Pioneers!</i>) yet still left you feeling as if you'd breezed through a slim short story. You wanted <i>more</i>. Cloudstreet was 400+ pages but felt more like 800 as it became mired in its own grit and finishing it was like freeing myself from quicksand.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7805511654183261505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1557804945" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1557804945" />
    <a title="Larry Deaton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2980533-larry-deaton"><img alt="Larry Deaton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367872796p2/2980533.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1557804945?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larry Deaton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larry Deaton" href="/user/show/2980533-larry-deaton">Larry Deaton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1557804945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1616694810551362876">I won't even attempt to tell the story that is in this book. Let's just say two families share a house in Perth, Australia for several decades. Time passes, the people change, and the House continues.<br/><br/>It's just a stunning book in its use of language, development of characters, and the story itself. The word unforgettable is used too much for many books, but Cloudstreet is exactly that. I believe I will remember this book for the rest of my life. Some of the minor characters, ones who are non-fami</span>
  <span id="freeText1616694810551362876" style="display:none">I won't even attempt to tell the story that is in this book. Let's just say two families share a house in Perth, Australia for several decades. Time passes, the people change, and the House continues.<br /><br />It's just a stunning book in its use of language, development of characters, and the story itself. The word unforgettable is used too much for many books, but Cloudstreet is exactly that. I believe I will remember this book for the rest of my life. Some of the minor characters, ones who are non-family members but who are "romantic" interests, are almost as memorable as the main characters.<br /><br />Not all the book works equally well. There are some paragraphs and passages that are just stunningly good. But then there are others that are a bit less than that. I like the way that Winton handles dialogue, but it takes a little work sometimes to figure out who said something; however, if you're willing to do that work, this book is one that repays the effort. <br /><br />I like the way time passes in this book also and the way Perth and Australia change. The sense of isolation, the wilderness that is Western Australia, and the House, of course, are always there.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1616694810551362876" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6434826778508723406"><br/>Lamb and Pickles? <br/><br/>The story is about two very dysfunctional Australian working class families...the Lamb's and the Pickles' during the 1940's-60's.<br/>This story evokes such a wide range of emotions from the reader...often in quick succession...that it is at times quite draining.   <span id="freeText6434826778508723406" style="display:none"><br />Lamb and Pickles? <br /><br />The story is about two very dysfunctional Australian working class families...the Lamb's and the Pickles' during the 1940's-60's.<br />This story evokes such a wide range of emotions from the reader...often in quick succession...that it is at times quite draining. I had to take regular breaks just to take a few deep breaths before I could go on.<br />Brilliantly told in such a way as to make you feel like you are right there...wanting to scream along with them or at them! It is a very emotive and thought provoking tale of two families struggling to get by in times of incredible hardship whilst also facing the every day demands of survival in some very challenging and varied sets of circumstances.<br /><br />Tears and laughter. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6434826778508723406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3305914101" />

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28967523-claire-fuller?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28967523-claire-fuller?shelf=1950s">1950s</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3305914101" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1944490562772014853">I know there's a lot of love for this book and aspects of the writing were tremendous. The way Winton creates new and resonant words, and wonderful rhythms: 'a booklumpy bag', 'the immodest backs of the oilslicked women', 'the sky was the colour of kerosene' - it sometimes reminded me of Under Milk Wood. But, you know when an author over-uses a word and then you can't stop seeing it. In this book Winton loves 'mob'. It's used about every five pages and it got so I was looking out for it. But tha</span>
  <span id="freeText1944490562772014853" style="display:none">I know there's a lot of love for this book and aspects of the writing were tremendous. The way Winton creates new and resonant words, and wonderful rhythms: 'a booklumpy bag', 'the immodest backs of the oilslicked women', 'the sky was the colour of kerosene' - it sometimes reminded me of Under Milk Wood. But, you know when an author over-uses a word and then you can't stop seeing it. In this book Winton loves 'mob'. It's used about every five pages and it got so I was looking out for it. But that's a silly thing; something that should have been caught in the copy editing. My bigger problem was the story. Two large families come together and live in a ramshackle house in Perth, and we see them grow up and older over the space of twenty years. Winton clearly, deliberately writes the novel in a kind of ramshackle, crazy way: flitting from one character to the next, not often stopping to rest or let me catch breath, and I simply didn't enjoy this. It meant that many of the characters (Hat, Red, Lon etc) were shadowy - not fully developed, and I didn't really care about any of them. It begins to settle onto Rose's and Quick's story as the book progresses, but this happened too late for me to worry about their fate.<br /><br />And one more thing... I get highly irritated by writers / screenwriters who name genital anatomy incorrectly. Winton - sorry to have to tell you, but it's not possible to see the notch in the top of someone's vagina. You wouldn't mistake a penis for a testicle. Please get it right.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1944490562772014853" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2038727564" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2038727564" />
    <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius"><img alt="Deborah Ideiosepius" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425943228p2/4802663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2038727564?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Ideiosepius" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius">Deborah Ideiosepius</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143205821" href="/book/show/12070806-cloudstreet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=2019-reading-challenge">2019-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=australiana">australiana</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=wordpress">wordpress</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=australian-authors">australian-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2038727564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13687973419852001926">Hailed as a classic, award winning, in the top ten of Dymocks 'book lovers best' and yet STILL a fine book!<br/><br/>Tim Winton once again breaks boundaries, because in terms of enjoyment in reading, for me classics are hit and miss while award winners are disappointingly, more often than not boring and unenjoyable to read.<br/><br/>Cloudstreet was an amazing, wow factor book, it sucked me in kept me under and I enjoyed it thoroughly. While it was written and published in the late 80's- early 90's neither the writi</span>
  <span id="freeText13687973419852001926" style="display:none">Hailed as a classic, award winning, in the top ten of Dymocks 'book lovers best' and yet STILL a fine book!<br /><br />Tim Winton once again breaks boundaries, because in terms of enjoyment in reading, for me classics are hit and miss while award winners are disappointingly, more often than not boring and unenjoyable to read.<br /><br />Cloudstreet was an amazing, wow factor book, it sucked me in kept me under and I enjoyed it thoroughly. While it was written and published in the late 80's- early 90's neither the writing style nor the context have dated in any way since the story takes place in the early to mid 1900's.<br /><br />Two different families, coming from two different places end up living under the same roof. A huge old house on Cloud Street in Perth. It is owned by the Pickles family, left to Sam Pickles in his brothers will. With the caveat that it cannot be sold for twenty years. Sam is wholly addicted to gambling, and his family is broke and dysfunctional. Since he can't make money without gambling it away, Sam rents half the house to the Lambs. The Lambs are a large family and as broke as the Pickles, with even worse luck in some ways. They lost their farm, went broke and one of their children has brain damage - but they are very different from the Pickels and start a grocery shop in the front room to make money.<br /><br />The whole chaotic mess of two families worth of individuals all living together and yet each of them a total island inside themselves is what Cloudstreet the novel is. Moving from one person to another, one story to the next, watching the children grow and the parents age, this is the slow undulation of reading that this books takes you on. It is a large book; 560 pages, so it has ups and downs on the reading path. For example, I was fascinated and hooked at the start, where we first meet the two families and see how they come to Cloud Street, but there was a patch after that that I was less thrilled by, I enjoyed it but it was more of a 3 than a 5 star. Then it picks up around the middle and for the rest of the book the reading was a breathless immersion experience.<br /><br />While Cloudstreet the book is clearly, unmistakably a Tim Winton book, it is very different from the most recent ones he has written. The Australian voices and attitudes are strong in it; SO strong, that if they were actors I would feel that they were overacting. Of course, these are voices of almost eighty years ago, they ARE different from modern Australian voices and I think it is a mark of the authors brilliance how differentiated the characters in his books can be. This ability to make characters as real as actors on a stage is part of what I think of as Winton's great talent and the strong inner lives of his characters are part of that. Most of Winton's characters are not especially happy and Cloustreet's people are no exception. They have inner hopes and fears, inner desperation and desolation - most of which are unshared with the people around them. These inner landscapes are what make the people so real. However a very strange thing happens with the people of Cloudstreet, they may be unhappy and lost but we, as readers are not. Now I like characters I can relate to, but the downside of that is if they are unhappy I get that way too which makes for an exhausting reading experience. Not here! The takeaway feeling is a gentle regret for peoples desolation, a feeling of encouragement that if people can't all be perfect then at least they can be the best they can and a sense of humour at people's unexplained foibles. Brilliant.<br /><br />So, when I sat down to write this review, I thought I wouldn't know what to say or that it had all been said before. Turns out I was mistaken about this. So glad I finally read this book that has been languishing on my to-read shelf for a while.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13687973419852001926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4208558704800933400">I finished reading this a couple of days ago and am still feeling the murky, underlying strangeness of <b>Cloudstreet</b> and Winton’s prose. He captures the human condition so well and the connectivity between the larger world and every tiny, insignificant one of us. <br/> <br/>This is the story of two down and out families weaved with an ever present inexplicability that mimics, perhaps, the entirely unexplainable nature of human beings. Why do we do the things we do, year after year, generation after helpless</span>
  <span id="freeText4208558704800933400" style="display:none">I finished reading this a couple of days ago and am still feeling the murky, underlying strangeness of <b>Cloudstreet</b> and Winton’s prose. He captures the human condition so well and the connectivity between the larger world and every tiny, insignificant one of us. <br /> <br />This is the story of two down and out families weaved with an ever present inexplicability that mimics, perhaps, the entirely unexplainable nature of human beings. Why do we do the things we do, year after year, generation after helpless generation? <br /><br />These people are down and dirty. They struggle, they fail. They are their own downfalls. Some of them die lonely, some of them escape. None of them are left unscarred. <br /><br /><b>Cloudstreet</b> is all of our childhood homes. It's a nostalgia for a time and place forever lost and out of reach; a time that never was. These people hurt forever. Just like us. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3031831141" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3031831141" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5346172-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1322945905p2/5346172.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3031831141?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/5346172-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330322690" href="/book/show/843996.Cloudstreet">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3031831141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15417201251059672391">"Life was something you didn't argue with, because when it came down to it, whether you barracked God or nothing at all, life was all there was. And death."<br/><br/>In this novel the author introduces the reader to two dysfunctional working class families, the Pickles and the Lambs, who flee the city after separate catastrophes and find refuge under the same roof. <br/><br/>Sam Pickles, who recently lost most of a hand in a boating mishap, inherits the sizeable, but ramshackle house on Cloudstreet, however a coven</span>
  <span id="freeText15417201251059672391" style="display:none">"Life was something you didn't argue with, because when it came down to it, whether you barracked God or nothing at all, life was all there was. And death."<br /><br />In this novel the author introduces the reader to two dysfunctional working class families, the Pickles and the Lambs, who flee the city after separate catastrophes and find refuge under the same roof. <br /><br />Sam Pickles, who recently lost most of a hand in a boating mishap, inherits the sizeable, but ramshackle house on Cloudstreet, however a covenant in the will prevents him from reselling the property for twenty years. Sam Pickles an inveterate gambler, but not a lucky one who invariably squanders any money that he makes, decides do take in tenants and rent out half of the building. <br /><br />The Lambs, whose farm has gone bankrupt and whose son Samson, nicknamed Fish, was recently left permanently brain-damaged in a fishing accident, decide to take up tenancy in the house. Just like the Pickles the Lambs have suffered their own run of bad luck but unlike their landlords are God-fearing and industrious. Determined to change their luck with hard work the Lambs establish a small thriving shop out of a ground floor room in their half of the house. An uneasy détente forms between the two families which with the passage of time gradually becomes begrudging tolerance, until finally, after an inter-family marriage, cooperation. <br /><br />Each of the main characters are fighting their own demons, Sam his gambling addiction, his wife Rose alcoholism and a liking of extra-marital sex, Oriel Lamb feels so unsettled by the house moves out into a tent in the back garden, Quick Lamb, Fish’s brother, battles with guilt over his perceived role in his sibling’s disability as does his father, whilst Rose Pickles resents being forced to quit schooling because of her parents's irresponsibility. <br /><br />Spanning a period of roughly twenty years from the end of WWII to the early 1960' the two families are afflicted by a number of tragic and horrific events—adultery, death, guilt, physical and mental disability to name but a few that when the author introduces a mass murder into the plot it seems almost trivial. <br /><br />Much of the book deals with what Sam refers to the "shifty shadow" of fate yet despite or because of their struggles the two families display a remarkable sense of dogged determination to succeed and in doing so becomes a tale about coping, acceptance and forgiveness, in particular the hardest form of all, self-forgiveness. Interspersed between the tragic elements there is also humour and magic, a talking pig and a mysterious Aborigine who can walk on water to name but a couple, which help lighten the mood. Although I must admit I couldn't always fathom the significance of, in particular, the magical interludes but then maybe that was just me. <br /><br />On the whole I found this is a quite remarkable book featuring a cast of interesting characters who are well drawn and who display a certain honesty, meaning that I found it an enjoyable read despite its seemingly bleak outlook.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15417201251059672391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2453337700" />
    <a title="Lo O&#39;Neill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61150031-lo-o-neill"><img alt="Lo O&#39;Neill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522751586p2/61150031.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2453337700?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lo O&#39;Neill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lo O&#39;Neill" href="/user/show/61150031-lo-o-neill">Lo O&#39;Neill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/24771460-cloudstreet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61150031-lo-o-neill?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61150031-lo-o-neill?shelf=book-club-reads">book-club-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61150031-lo-o-neill?shelf=historial-fiction">historial-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2453337700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14659223758223036437">This would not normally be a book that I would have picked up but my work book club chose it for our read of the month, so I found myself starting into this and I am conflicted regarding my thoughts on the book. To tell you the truth, I did debate not finishing it a couple of time. <br/><br/>Winton’s development of the characters was wonderful, they progress gradually and you constantly feel like you are getting to know them more throughout the book, which I know sounds obvious but there are some authors </span>
  <span id="freeText14659223758223036437" style="display:none">This would not normally be a book that I would have picked up but my work book club chose it for our read of the month, so I found myself starting into this and I am conflicted regarding my thoughts on the book. To tell you the truth, I did debate not finishing it a couple of time. <br /><br />Winton’s development of the characters was wonderful, they progress gradually and you constantly feel like you are getting to know them more throughout the book, which I know sounds obvious but there are some authors that once they set the ground work it is all about the plot, but not this one, it really is a character driven novel. The only problem with that was that I didn’t really connect with any of the characters which really made this a tough read to get through, especially sense the novel really didn’t have a plot or big build up to something. Just the viewing of the lives of two families that coexist in the same house/back garden.<br /> <br />After finishing this book I was with a sense that this story would have had more resonance with me if I was Australian, but due to being Irish there was a lot of things that I fundamentally struggled to understand about post war Perth – which has set me on a little tangent of looking into Australian history as I have realized a shocking lack of knowledge on the subject! <br /><br />The story itself is a gritty, family epic that is beautifully written and I can see why people are raving about Winton’s writing in this book, but I still struggled to really love the novel. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14659223758223036437" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2383395105" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2383395105" />
    <a title="Tania" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6450088-tania"><img alt="Tania" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603638299p2/6450088.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2383395105?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tania" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tania" href="/user/show/6450088-tania">Tania</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2383395105" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4842836210906109021"><blockquote>We all turn into the same thing, don't we? Memories, shadows, worries, dreams. We all join up somewhere in the end.</blockquote><br/>3.5 stars. I know I'm the odd one out here, as almost all my GR friends gave this 5 stars. So let me start of by saying that I strongly suspect that this was a case of the the right book at the wrong time. <br/>The author's writing is superb, I was especially impressed with the fact that he can use very different writing styles in the same story. Although I grew to love the characters, e</span>
  <span id="freeText4842836210906109021" style="display:none"><blockquote>We all turn into the same thing, don't we? Memories, shadows, worries, dreams. We all join up somewhere in the end.</blockquote><br />3.5 stars. I know I'm the odd one out here, as almost all my GR friends gave this 5 stars. So let me start of by saying that I strongly suspect that this was a case of the the right book at the wrong time. <br />The author's writing is superb, I was especially impressed with the fact that he can use very different writing styles in the same story. Although I grew to love the characters, especially Oriel and Lester, it took me quite a while to get into the story. I will definitely try more books by this author as I now have a better idea of what to expect - this is not a quick, easy read, but something that you need to take your time with. You should be in a space where you can immerse yourself in the characters, and their very unique and beautifully described world/home. Sometimes funny, sometimes sad - this story, with it's hints of magic, is threaded throughout with of yearning to belong, to find your place. <br /><br />The Story: From separate catastrophes two rural families flee to the city and find themselves sharing a great, breathing, shuddering joint called Cloudstreet, where they begin their lives again from scratch.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4842836210906109021" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_8407173'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8407173-jasper-jones"><img alt="Jasper Jones" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1275886753l/8407173._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5dvR58qvU9Wv094gwJ4G9pTapzMGYtN1BIhwqosZdK3f/bg1cfGavQj4akE3awwpQoVA+8jvXRJf9jYbRlqGNw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('B8R+Vlvw/UH5rSNUi6rOFA5aqS9LgAiFDBLJF0muU/w94heE4K40KV6GlzV8X8TL2AVO54UNhuJXbI+mhO2hZg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/19929.Tim_Winton">About Tim Winton</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/19929.Tim_Winton"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1240750986p3/19929.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/19929.Tim_Winton">Tim Winton
</a></div>

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8070704569263782929">Tim Winton was born in Perth, Western Australia, but moved at a young age to the small country town of Albany.<br/><br/>While a student at Curtin University of Technology, Winton wrote his first novel, An Open Swimmer. It went on to win The Australian/Vogel Literary Award in 1981, and launched his writing career. In fact, he wrote "the best part of three books while at university". His second book, Shallows</span>
  <span id="freeText8070704569263782929" style="display:none">Tim Winton was born in Perth, Western Australia, but moved at a young age to the small country town of Albany.<br /><br />While a student at Curtin University of Technology, Winton wrote his first novel, An Open Swimmer. It went on to win The Australian/Vogel Literary Award in 1981, and launched his writing career. In fact, he wrote "the best part of three books while at university". His second book, Shallows, won the Miles Franklin Award in 1984. It wasn't until Cloudstreet was published in 1991, however, that his career and economic future were cemented.<br /><br />In 1995 Winton’s novel, The Riders, was shortlisted for the Booker Prize, as was his 2002 book, Dirt Music. Both are currently being adapted for film. He has won many other prizes, including the Miles Franklin Award three times: for Shallows (1984), Cloudstreet (1992) and Dirt Music (2002). Cloudstreet is arguably his best-known work, regularly appearing in lists of Australia’s best-loved novels. His latest novel, released in 2013, is called Eyrie.<br /><br />He is now one of Australia's most esteemed novelists, writing for both adults and children. All his books are still in print and have been published in eighteen different languages. His work has also been successfully adapted for stage, screen and radio. On the publication of his novel, Dirt Music, he collaborated with broadcaster, Lucky Oceans, to produce a compilation CD, Dirt Music – Music for a Novel.<br /><br />He has lived in Italy, France, Ireland and Greece but currently lives in Western Australia with his wife and three children.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8070704569263782929" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
